Refutation of the Mu'tazila Interpretation of 'Yurīdu an Yughwiyakum'
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of Q. 19:59 and criticizes 'Abd al-Jabbar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila interpretation of Qur'an 19:59 as 'He wants to punish you' leads to logical contradictions regarding divine will and why Nasafi criticizes 'Abd al-Jabbar for accepting such a weak interpretation.

Core Idea
Interpreting 'yughwiyakum' as 'punish' creates an impossible dilemma about God's will.

This passage finds Nasafi engaging directly with a rival theological school. The Mu'tazila attempted to soften a verse that seems to attribute leading astray to God by reinterpreting the key word. Nasafi shows that their rescue attempt only leads to deeper problems, and he sharply criticizes a leading Mu'tazili scholar for being satisfied with such flimsy reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila interpretation
Read how the Mu'tazila interpret 'yurīdu an yughwiyakum' as 'He wants to punish you' and their proof from the use of 'ghayy' in another verse.
This interpretation attempts to avoid the implication that God wills to lead people astray.
2
Analyze Nasafi's refutation
Follow Nasafi's dilemma: if God wants to punish them, does He want their disbelief or their belief? Both options lead to contradictions.
3
Reflect on the critique of 'Abd al-Jabbar
Consider why Nasafi finds it astonishing that a leading scholar would accept such a weak interpretation and what the closing proverb teaches about bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غيرهم أن المراد بقوله: ﴿ يُرِيدُ أَن يُغْوِيَكُمْ ﴾ أي يعاقبكم، وألغى يذكر ويراد به العذاب. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَسَوْفَ يَلْقَوْنَ غَيّا ) أي عذابا . وجوابه كجواب تأويل الجبائي أنه يريد أن يعاقبهم وهو يريد منهم الكفر أَوَيرِيدُ أن يعاقبهم وهو يريد منهم الإيمان؟ فبأي الجوابين أجاب فالكلام على ما ذكرنا ذكر هذه الوجوه وحكى كل تأويل عن صاحبه (۲) عبد الجبار الرازي(۳) ثم قال : وكل هذا واضح. فإن أراد أنه واضح وجه بطلانه فهو كما أراد، وإن أراد أنه واضح وجه صحته فهو يقول بقول يجب به الاستحياء من الناس (٤) والاستغفار من الله تعالى، والتعجب من رجل يدعي الترأس على أهل نحلته والتبحر في علوم أصحابه. ثم يرضى بمثل هذه التأويلات مع وهائها وظهور فسادها لأول الفكرة، وما أصدق ما قيل: حبك الشيء يعمي ويصم .
The others claim that the meaning of His statement 'He wants to lead you astray' (yurīdu an yughwiyakum) is 'He wants to punish you,' and they mention that 'ghayy' is used to mean punishment. Allah Almighty said: 'Then they will meet ghayy' – meaning punishment. Its answer is like the answer to al-Jubba'i's interpretation: that He wants to punish them while He wants from them disbelief, or does He want to punish them while He wants from them faith? Whichever of the two answers he gives, the discourse is as we have mentioned. He mentioned these aspects and narrated each interpretation from its proponent – 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi – then he said: 'All this is clear.' If he meant that the aspect of its falsehood is clear, then it is as he intended. If he meant that the aspect of its correctness is clear, then he holds a statement that necessitates feeling ashamed before people and seeking forgiveness from Allah Almighty. And it is astonishing that a man who claims leadership over the people of his sect and depth in the sciences of his followers is then satisfied with such interpretations, despite their weakness and the obviousness of their corruption at first thought. How true is the saying: 'Your love of something blinds and deafens.'
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Nasafi begins by reporting the Mu'tazila interpretation of the verse 'He wants to lead you astray' (Q. 19:59) as meaning 'He wants to punish you,' and they cite the verse 'Then they will meet ghayy' meaning punishment. Nasafi then applies the same argument he used against al-Jubba'i: either God wants to punish them while wanting them to disbelieve (which would mean He wills both faith and disbelief?), or He wants to punish them while wanting them to believe (which would contradict the punishment). Whichever answer they give leads to inconsistency. He states that these interpretations were narrated from 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi, who claimed they are 'clear.' Nasafi responds sharply: if he meant the falsehood is clear, then correct; but if he meant the correctness is clear, then he holds a position that requires shame before people and seeking forgiveness from God. Nasafi expresses astonishment that someone who claims leadership and deep knowledge would be satisfied with such flimsy interpretations whose corruption is obvious from first thought. He concludes with the proverb 'Love of something blinds and deafens,' implying that partisan bias leads to intellectual blindness.

Key Terms
يغوي
غيا
الجبائي
عبد الجبار الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'yughwiyakum' can legitimately be interpreted as 'He wants to punish you' based on the use of 'ghayy' as punishment.
Correction: While 'ghayy' can mean punishment in some contexts, interpreting 'yurīdu an yughwiyakum' as 'He wants to punish you' creates an impossible dilemma about God's simultaneous will for disbelief or belief, as shown by Nasafi.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the fundamental problem with interpreting 'يغوي' as 'punishes'?
A) It contradicts the Arabic language.
B) It leads to contradictions regarding divine will — either God wills disbelief or belief.
C) It makes God responsible for evil.
D) It is not supported by any early commentator.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila interpretation of 'yughwiyakum' as 'punish' is rationally untenable because it forces a contradiction regarding God's will, and Nasafi's critique highlights the danger of allowing partisan bias to override sound reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'yurīdu an yughwiyakum', do you feel a tension in your heart? What does that tension reveal about your understanding of God's will and human responsibility?
?
Nasafi criticizes 'Abd al-Jabbar for being satisfied with a weak interpretation due to love of his sect. Is there any belief or practice in your own life that you cling to because of emotional attachment rather than clear evidence?
?
The proverb says love blinds and deafens. When has your love for a person, group, or idea prevented you from seeing the truth? Will you today ask Allah to protect you from that blindness?

Mu'tazila Objection to the Rational Proof of God's Will
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila argument that the will in the verses is a will of compulsion, and lack of occurrence does not indicate weakness if God has the power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila reinterpretation of divine will as compulsion and their argument that non-occurrence of what is willed does not imply impotence.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila defend human freedom by redefining God's will in the verses as a will to compel, not a will of choice.

Having established the rational proof that God's will is always effective, we now encounter a major objection from the Mu'tazila. They argue that the verses do not prove effective will but rather a hypothetical will of compulsion. This lesson unpacks their argument and the distinction they draw between power and actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
First, read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that the objection has two parts: one directed at the verses and one at the rational proof. The passage is concise, so take your time.
Pay attention to the key term 'mashī'at al-jabr' (will of compulsion).
2
Understand 'will of compulsion'
The Mu'tazila claim that when God says 'if your Lord willed, they would have believed', the will here is a will to compel them forcibly, not a will that their free choice aligns with guidance. This protects human free will.
They do not deny God's will; they reinterpret its application.
3
Analyze the rational argument
The Mu'tazila argue that non-occurrence of what is willed only indicates weakness if God lacks the power to bring it about. Since God has the power to compel faith in everyone, His not doing so does not prove weakness. Thus, the rational proof is invalid.
This distinction between power (qudra) and actualization is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض للمعتزلة] فاعترضت المعتزلة على هذه الآيات وعلى هذا المعقول، فزعمت أن المراد من المشيئة المذكورة في الآيات مشيئة الجبر أي لو شاء ربك لجبرهم على الهدى ولآمنوا جبرًا وما أشركوا ، وبهذا اعترضوا أيضا على المعقول فإنهم يقولون: انعدام ما يشاء أو وجود ما لا يشاء إنما يدل على الضعف أن لو لم تكن له قدرة إيجاد (ما يشاء ودفع ما لا يشاء وله قدرة إيجاد (۱) (۱) إيمان كل كافر (۱) جبرًا منه. وقدرة دفع كل كفر جبرًا منه، ومن هذا وصفه لا يوصف بالضعف.
Objection of the Mu'tazila: And the Mu'tazila objected to these verses and to this rational proof. They claimed that the meaning of the will mentioned in the verses is the will of compulsion (mashī'at al-jabr), i.e., if your Lord willed, He would have compelled them to guidance, and they would have believed under compulsion, and they would not have associated partners. And with this they also objected to the rational proof, for they say: The absence of what is willed or the existence of what is not willed only indicates weakness if one does not have the power to bring about what one wills and to repel what one does not will. And He has the power to bring about (1)(1) the faith of every disbeliever (1) through compulsion, and the power to repel every disbelief through compulsion. And with this description, He is not described with weakness.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Mu'tazila against both the previously cited verses and the rational proof. The Mu'tazila argue that the 'will' (mashī'ah) mentioned in verses like 'If your Lord willed, they would have believed' refers to a 'will of compulsion' (mashī'at al-jabr). That is, if God willed, He would compel them to believe, but He chooses not to. So the verses do not prove that God's will is always effective in the actual world. They also object to the rational proof by making a distinction: the non-occurrence of what is willed or the occurrence of what is not willed only indicates weakness if the being lacks the power to bring about what He wills and to prevent what He does not will. Since God does have the power to create faith in every disbeliever through compulsion and to prevent all disbelief through compulsion, His not doing so does not indicate weakness. Therefore, the rational proof is invalid because it assumes that lack of occurrence implies impotence, but God's power remains intact. This objection is typical of the Mu'tazila, who affirm human freedom and argue that God does not will evil actions in a way that compels them.

Key Terms
مشيئة الجبر
جبر
قدرة
إيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazila completely deny God's will or power, but they only reinterpret the type of will and affirm God's power.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm God's power but argue that His will in these verses is conditional compulsion, not a will that necessitates free response.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what kind of will is attributed to God in the verses about guidance?
A) Will of choice (mashī'at al-ikhtiyār).
B) Will of compulsion (mashī'at al-jabr).
C) Will of permission (mashī'at al-idhn).
D) Will of decree (mashī'at al-qadā').

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that God's will in the verses is a will of compulsion, and that non-occurrence does not indicate weakness because God has the power to compel.

?
God has the power to make you a perfect believer without any effort on your part, yet He doesn't. Do you see His restraint as an invitation to strive, or do you take it as permission to slack? Reflect on one area where you have been complacent in faith because 'God could fix it anyway'. What will you actively do this week to take charge of your own iman?

Mu'tazilite Objection on Divine Will as Compulsion and Its Refutation
Nasafi records and refutes the Mu'tazilite objection regarding divine will as compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that God's will in certain verses refers to compulsion, and how Nasafi refutes it using their own principles, exposing a contradiction in their position.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites' interpretation of divine will as compulsion collapses on their own doctrine of human agency.

In this passage, Nasafi records a key Mu'tazilite objection to the verses that seem to indicate God's will is always effective. They reinterpret the will as 'will of compulsion' (jabr) to avoid the conclusion that God could force faith but does not. Nasafi then refutes their interpretation by turning their own principles against them, exposing a contradiction in their position. This lesson unpacks this debate and the logical consequences of the Mu'tazilite stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim that 'will' in the verses means compulsion, and that God's failure to compel does not indicate weakness.
2
Examine the answer's request
Nasafi asks them to define 'will of compulsion', highlighting their internal disagreement.
Notice that the Mu'tazilites themselves differ in interpretation.
3
Analyze Abu Hudhayl's view
He says compulsion means God creates faith in people without their choice.
4
Follow the refutation
Nasafi uses the Mu'tazilite principle that the agent of an act is its doer to show that if God creates faith, He becomes the believer, not the person.
This directly contradicts their own earlier stance on human agency.
5
Grasp the reductio
The absurd conclusion that even if faith were an acquisition (kasb), the servant would still not be the believer—this shows the flaw in their reasoning.
Also note the sarcastic remark about the Mu'tazilites' shame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعترضت المعتزلة على هذه الآيات وعلى هذا المعقول، فزعمت أن المراد من المشيئة المذكورة في الآيات مشيئة الجبر أي لو شاء ربك لجبرهم على الهدى ولآمنوا جبرًا وما أشركوا ، وبهذا اعترضوا أيضا على المعقول فإنهم يقولون: انعدام ما يشاء أو وجود ما لا يشاء إنما يدل على الضعف أن لو لم تكن له قدرة إيجاد ما يشاء ودفع ما لا يشاء وله قدرة إيجاد إيمان كل كافر جبرًا منه. وقدرة دفع كل كفر جبرًا منه، ومن هذا وصفه لا يوصف بالضعف. والجواب عنه أن نقول : أيش تعنون بمشيئة القصر والجبر؟ فتفرقوا عند ذلك في تفسيرها، فزعم أبو الهذيل ومن تابعه أن تفسير ذلك بأن يخلق فيهم الإيمان جبرا بدون اختيارهم فيوجد فيهم الإيمان ويندفع الكفر. قيل لهم: إن من مذهبكم أن المؤمن هو فاعل الإيمان والكافر هو فاعل الكفر، ولهذا أبيتم أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لأفعال الخلق لأنه لو كان خالقا لكان هو المؤمن الكافر المطيع العاصي المصلي الصائم، فعلى هذا لو خلق فيهم الإيمان لكان هو المؤمن لا العباد فلا يتصور إيمانهم ولم تنفذ مشيئة فبطل قوله: ﴿فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَاكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا ﴾ إذ له قدرة جعل نفسه مؤمنًا لا قدرة جعل الكافر مؤمنًا وكذا ما اندفع عنه العجز عن جعل الكافر مؤمناً بل له القدرة على جعل نفسه مؤمناً فلو شاء لأمن بنفسه وأتى نفسه هداها لا غير، ومن العجب العجيب أنه لو خلق في العبد إيمانا كسبا له باختياره وتعلقت قدرته به لم يكن العبد مؤمناً بل كان الله تعالى به مؤمنًا، لأنه هو الذي أوجد الإيمان ولو خلق فيه إيمانا وهدى بلا اختيار من جهة العبد ولا اكتساب ولا تعلق قدرته به لكان العبد مؤمناً، ولو كان الله تعالى أشرك المعتزلة في ربوبيته وفوض إليهم نصب أدلة دينه وعلق ذلك باختيارهم فأوجب على جميع الأمم الانقياد إليهم والرضا لصنيعهم والقبول لتحكمهم لكان الأولى بهم أن يستحيوا عن مثل هذا الكلام ونصب مثل هذا الدليل، فكيف ولم يوجد شيء من ذلك، فلما رأى الجبائي عوار هذا الكلام وتشنيع أهل الحق عليهم زعم أن تفسير مشيئة الجبر
The Mu'tazilites objected to these verses and to this rational argument, claiming that what is meant by the will mentioned in the verses is the will of compulsion (jabr), i.e., if your Lord willed, He would have compelled them to guidance and they would have believed by compulsion and would not have associated partners. And by this they also objected to the rational argument, for they say: The non-existence of what He wills or the existence of what He does not will only indicates weakness if He did not have the power to bring about what He wills and to repel what He does not will, but He has the power to bring about the faith of every disbeliever by compulsion from Him, and the power to repel every disbelief by compulsion from Him, and from this, He is not described as weak. The answer to it is that we say: What do you mean by the will of restriction and compulsion? They then differed in its interpretation. Abu Hudhayl and those who followed him claimed that its interpretation is that He creates faith in them by compulsion without their choice, so faith comes to be in them and disbelief is repelled. It was said to them: It is from your doctrine that the believer is the doer of faith and the disbeliever is the doer of disbelief, and for this reason you refused that Allah is the creator of the actions of creatures, because if He were the creator, He would be the believer, the disbeliever, the obedient, the disobedient, the praying, the fasting. Based on this, if He created faith in them, He would be the believer, not the servants, so their faith would not be conceivable and the will would not be effective, and thus His statement {If He willed, He would have guided you all together} and His statement {And if We willed, We would have given every soul its guidance} would be invalid, for He has the power to make Himself a believer, not the power to make the disbeliever a believer. And likewise, the inability to make the disbeliever a believer is not repelled from Him; rather, He has the power to make Himself a believer, so if He willed, He would believe by Himself and would give Himself its guidance, not others. And it is among the strangest wonders that if He created in the servant faith as an acquisition (kasb) by his choice and his power was attached to it, the servant would not be a believer; rather, Allah would be a believer through it, because He is the one who brought faith into existence. But if He created in him faith and guidance without any choice from the servant, nor acquisition, nor attachment of his power to it, the servant would be a believer. And if Allah had made the Mu'tazilites partners in His lordship and delegated to them the erection of the proofs of His religion and made that contingent upon their choice, and obliged all nations to submit to them, to be pleased with their actions, and to accept their judgments, then they would have been more entitled to be ashamed of such speech and the erection of such a proof. But how can that be when none of that has occurred? When al-Jubba'i saw the defectiveness of this speech and the rebuke of the people of truth upon them, he claimed that the interpretation of the will of compulsion...
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. First, the Mu'tazilites object to the Qur'anic verses and the rational argument by reinterpreting 'will' as 'will of compulsion' (mashī'at al-jabr), meaning that if God had willed universal faith, He would have compelled people, which He did not do. They argue that the absence of something willed does not indicate weakness if one has the power to bring it about but chooses not to, so God remains powerful. The answer begins by asking them to define what they mean by 'will of compulsion'. The Mu'tazilites differ among themselves. Abu Hudhayl's view is that it means God creates faith in people by force without their choice. The refutation uses the Mu'tazilites' own principles: they hold that the agent of an act is its doer. Therefore, if God created faith in people, He would be the believer, not the people. This would nullify the meaning of the verses, because then God would be making Himself a believer, not the disbelievers. The author then presents a reductio: if God created faith as an acquired act (kasb) with the servant's choice and power, the servant would still not be the true agent—God would be. But if God created faith without any involvement from the servant, then the servant would be a believer (contradicting the Mu'tazilite claim that faith requires human agency). This shows the absurdity of their position. The passage ends with a sarcastic remark: if God had delegated religious authority to the Mu'tazilites, they should be ashamed of such arguments. Finally, it mentions that al-Jubba'i recognized the weakness of the earlier Mu'tazilite position and proposed another interpretation, but this is cut off.

أبو الهذيل
كسب
قدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites think that by interpreting divine will as 'will of compulsion', they can preserve human free will while avoiding the implication that God wills all events.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this interpretation leads to contradictions within their own system, because if God creates faith by compulsion, He becomes the believer, not the human, which undermines their principle of human agency.

Refuting Abu Hashim's Definition of Compulsion
Refutes Abu Hashim's definition of compulsion using examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hashim's claim that God creates necessary knowledge to compel faith is flawed.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge does not compel belief; even the obstinate disbelieve.

Abu Hashim attempted to define divine compulsion (jabr) as God creating necessary knowledge in people that punishment awaits if they do not believe. In this passage, the author refutes this by pointing to clear examples: the stubborn disbelievers and Iblis himself, who knew the consequences yet persisted in disbelief. This lesson examines why necessary knowledge does not force belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without deep analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents Abu Hashim's view and then immediately rejects it.
The passage uses a concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the examples of the stubborn people and Iblis. Ask yourself: what did they know, and what did they do?
These examples are central to the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that if necessary knowledge compelled belief, the stubborn people and Iblis would have believed. Since they did not, Abu Hashim's definition fails.
This refutation relies on observable historical examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابنه أبو هاشم فساد كلام أبيه زعم أن معنى مشيئة الجبر أن يخلق الله تعالى لهم العلم الضروري أنهم لو لم يؤمنوا لعذبوا عذابًا شديدا. وهذا أيضا فاسد لأن أهل العناد كانوا يعلمون أنهم يخلدون في النار بتكذيبه عليه السلام وما كانوا يؤمنون، وإبليس يعلم بذلك ولا يؤمن، فكذا من قامت له الدلائل الموجبة للعلم أو الذي خلق له العلم الضروري يعلم بذلك ولا يؤمن. ثم المذهب عنده أن الله تعالى يقدر على الظلم والسفه والكذب، ولو فعل شيئًا من ذلك لبطلت ألوهيته، ولا ضرر أعظم من زوال الربوبية وصيرورته عبدًا مربوبًا معاقبًا وهو يعلم أنه لو ظلم أو كذب أو سفه لتزول ربوبيته فكان على قضية كلامه مجبوراً على العدل والصدق والحكمة. وقد قررت الكلام فيه في مسألة خلق الأفعال ثم يقال له ولأبيه: هل بقيت معه قدرة الكفر بعد العلم الضروري بصحة الإيمان أو العلم أنه يعاقب؟ فإن قال: لا، قيل: كيف يؤمر بترك كفر لا قدرة له عليه؟ وكيف يؤمر بالإيمان بدون القدرة إذ لو كانت قدرة الإيمان موجودة لكانت هي بعينها قدرة الكفر؟ وكيف يعاقب على كفر لا قدرة له عليه؟ فإن قال: نعم. قيل: إذا كنت قدرة الكفر موجودة كان من الجائز أن يكفر بها ولا يؤمن.
His son Abu Hashim corrupts his father's statement, claiming that the meaning of the will of compulsion is that God Almighty creates for them necessary knowledge that if they did not believe, they would be severely punished. And this too is corrupt, because the stubborn people knew that they would abide forever in Hell for denying him, peace be upon him, and they did not believe, and Iblis knows that and does not believe. Likewise, whoever has evidence that necessitates knowledge, or whoever has necessary knowledge created for him, knows that and does not believe. Then the doctrine with him is that God Almighty is able to do injustice, foolishness, and lying, and if He did any of that, His divinity would be invalidated. And there is no greater harm than the removal of lordship and His becoming a servant, a subject, punished, while He knows that if He were unjust, a liar, or foolish, His lordship would cease, so according to his statement He is compelled to justice, truth, and wisdom. And I have already discussed the matter in the issue of the creation of actions. Then it is said to him and his father: Does the power of disbelief remain with him after the necessary knowledge of the truth of faith or the knowledge that he will be punished? If he says: No, it is said: How is he commanded to abandon disbelief for which he has no power? And how is he commanded to have faith without power, since if the power of faith existed, it would be the very power of disbelief? And how is he punished for disbelief for which he has no power? If he says: Yes, it is said: Since the power of disbelief exists, it is possible that he disbelieve by it and not believe.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim's theory of compulsory faith (jabr) and a logical dilemma about the persistence of power after necessary knowledge. The author first states Abu Hashim's claim that God's will of compulsion means He creates necessary knowledge in people that if they do not believe, they will be severely punished. The author refutes this by giving examples: the obstinate people knew they would be eternally punished for denying the prophet, yet they did not believe; similarly, Iblis knew but did not believe. Thus, even those with necessary knowledge or clear evidence can disbelieve. The author then critiques the view that God is able to do injustice but would not because it would void His divinity, effectively making Him compelled to be just. He then poses a dilemma to the proponents of jabr: after necessary knowledge of the truth or punishment, does the power to disbelieve remain? If no, then how can one be commanded to abandon disbelief or to believe without power? Moreover, if the power to believe exists, it is the same as the power to disbelieve. If yes, then it is possible to disbelieve, contradicting the claim of compulsion. This shows the logical inconsistency.

Key Terms
مشيئة الجبر
العلم الضروري
أهل العناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge forces belief.
Correction: The author shows that even with necessary knowledge, disbelief is possible, as seen in the examples of the stubborn and Iblis.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the stubborn people and Iblis demonstrate?
A) They believed after receiving necessary knowledge.
B) They disbelieved despite having necessary knowledge.
C) They had no knowledge of punishment.
D) They were compelled to believe by God.

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge does not compel belief; disbelief remains possible even with certainty of punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself knowing the truth of Islam and the consequences of disobedience, yet your heart remains cold or your actions disobedient? What does your inaction reveal about the state of your faith? This lesson shows that even necessary knowledge does not force belief—so what is holding you back from true surrender?
?
Consider the stubborn people and Iblis: they had undeniable knowledge, yet they chose disbelief. Is there an area in your life where you are willfully ignoring what you know to be true? What would it cost you to act on that knowledge today?

The author argues that evidence and necessary knowledge do not compel belief. If you are certain of the truth of Islam, why do you still harbor doubts or fail to obey? What does your hesitation reveal about your heart’s attachment to this world? Commit to one practical step toward acting on what you know, starting now.

intermediate

The Dilemma of Power and Accountability
Presents a logical dilemma about power after necessary knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that arises from claiming that after necessary knowledge the power to disbelieve is removed.

Core Idea
If power to disbelieve is removed, command and punishment become meaningless.

In the second part of this argument, the author presents a powerful dilemma to proponents of jabr. He asks: after necessary knowledge of the truth, does the power to disbelieve remain or not? Each answer leads to a contradiction, undermining the entire theory of compulsion. This lesson examines this logical trap.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the author first critiques the view about God, then poses a question about human power.
The dilemma has two clear horns.
2
Analyze the first horn (No power remains)
If the answer is no, ask yourself: How can one be commanded to abandon disbelief if he has no power over it? How can he be punished? The author lists three problems.
These problems highlight the injustice of commanding without power.
3
Analyze the second horn (Power remains)
If the answer is yes, then disbelief is possible, which directly contradicts the claim of compulsion.
This horn shows the theory's internal inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابنه أبو هاشم فساد كلام أبيه زعم أن معنى مشيئة الجبر أن يخلق الله تعالى لهم العلم الضروري أنهم لو لم يؤمنوا لعذبوا عذابًا شديدا. وهذا أيضا فاسد لأن أهل العناد كانوا يعلمون أنهم يخلدون في النار بتكذيبه عليه السلام وما كانوا يؤمنون، وإبليس يعلم بذلك ولا يؤمن، فكذا من قامت له الدلائل الموجبة للعلم أو الذي خلق له العلم الضروري يعلم بذلك ولا يؤمن. ثم المذهب عنده أن الله تعالى يقدر على الظلم والسفه والكذب، ولو فعل شيئًا من ذلك لبطلت ألوهيته، ولا ضرر أعظم من زوال الربوبية وصيرورته عبدًا مربوبًا معاقبًا وهو يعلم أنه لو ظلم أو كذب أو سفه لتزول ربوبيته فكان على قضية كلامه مجبوراً على العدل والصدق والحكمة. وقد قررت الكلام فيه في مسألة خلق الأفعال ثم يقال له ولأبيه: هل بقيت معه قدرة الكفر بعد العلم الضروري بصحة الإيمان أو العلم أنه يعاقب؟ فإن قال: لا، قيل: كيف يؤمر بترك كفر لا قدرة له عليه؟ وكيف يؤمر بالإيمان بدون القدرة إذ لو كانت قدرة الإيمان موجودة لكانت هي بعينها قدرة الكفر؟ وكيف يعاقب على كفر لا قدرة له عليه؟ فإن قال: نعم. قيل: إذا كنت قدرة الكفر موجودة كان من الجائز أن يكفر بها ولا يؤمن.
His son Abu Hashim corrupts his father's statement, claiming that the meaning of the will of compulsion is that God Almighty creates for them necessary knowledge that if they did not believe, they would be severely punished. And this too is corrupt, because the stubborn people knew that they would abide forever in Hell for denying him, peace be upon him, and they did not believe, and Iblis knows that and does not believe. Likewise, whoever has evidence that necessitates knowledge, or whoever has necessary knowledge created for him, knows that and does not believe. Then the doctrine with him is that God Almighty is able to do injustice, foolishness, and lying, and if He did any of that, His divinity would be invalidated. And there is no greater harm than the removal of lordship and His becoming a servant, a subject, punished, while He knows that if He were unjust, a liar, or foolish, His lordship would cease, so according to his statement He is compelled to justice, truth, and wisdom. And I have already discussed the matter in the issue of the creation of actions. Then it is said to him and his father: Does the power of disbelief remain with him after the necessary knowledge of the truth of faith or the knowledge that he will be punished? If he says: No, it is said: How is he commanded to abandon disbelief for which he has no power? And how is he commanded to have faith without power, since if the power of faith existed, it would be the very power of disbelief? And how is he punished for disbelief for which he has no power? If he says: Yes, it is said: Since the power of disbelief exists, it is possible that he disbelieve by it and not believe.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim's theory of compulsory faith (jabr) and a logical dilemma about the persistence of power after necessary knowledge. The author first states Abu Hashim's claim that God's will of compulsion means He creates necessary knowledge in people that if they do not believe, they will be severely punished. The author refutes this by giving examples: the obstinate people knew they would be eternally punished for denying the prophet, yet they did not believe; similarly, Iblis knew but did not believe. Thus, even those with necessary knowledge or clear evidence can disbelieve. The author then critiques the view that God is able to do injustice but would not because it would void His divinity, effectively making Him compelled to be just. He then poses a dilemma to the proponents of jabr: after necessary knowledge of the truth or punishment, does the power to disbelieve remain? If no, then how can one be commanded to abandon disbelief or to believe without power? Moreover, if the power to believe exists, it is the same as the power to disbelieve. If yes, then it is possible to disbelieve, contradicting the claim of compulsion. This shows the logical inconsistency.

Key Terms
قدرة الكفر
قدرة الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone claims that after necessary knowledge the power to disbelieve is removed, what problem arises?
A) He can still disbelieve.
B) Command and punishment become meaningless.
C) He becomes compelled to believe.
D) He loses free will.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma shows that compulsion theory cannot coherently account for command, power, and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin or neglect an obligation, do you instinctively blame your circumstances or your lack of power, rather than your own choice? How does this dilemma force you to confront the reality that you still had the power to do otherwise?
?
Have you ever excused your disobedience by saying 'God willed it' or 'I had no choice'? If you truly believe that the power to disbelieve remains even after knowing the truth, what does that mean for the excuses you make for your failures?
?
If you were to stand before God tomorrow and be asked about a sin you committed, would you dare to say 'I had no power to avoid it' after reading that even the power to disbelieve remains? How will you change your understanding of accountability starting today?

The Objection and the Ash‘ari and Maturidi Responses
Objection and two theological responses to divine pleasure with disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection about God's pleasure with disbelief is answered by both Ash‘ari and Maturidi theologians.

Core Idea
God disapproves of disbelief even as He wills its existence for just purposes.

This passage addresses a second objection raised by opponents: if God is not pleased with disbelief, how can He will it? The author presents two distinct responses—one from al-Ash‘ari and one from the Maturidi scholars—showing how each reconciles divine displeasure with the actual occurrence of disbelief. This lesson takes you through the logic of both responses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where the opponent asks: if God does not love disbelief, how can He will something that would make Him ignorant? Recognize the parallel to the first question.
The objection tries to create a contradiction between God's attributes and His actions.
2
See the Ash‘ari answer
Al-Ash‘ari says that God is pleased with disbelief as ugly, and the verse 'He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants' refers only to believers. See how this repels the objection.
This approach distinguishes between the act itself (ugly) and the divine pleasure as relating to the believer.
3
Compare with the Maturidi answer
The Maturidi scholars interpret pleasure as approval (istihsan). God does not approve of disbelief; He disapproves and hates it. Disbelief exists only as a cause for punishment, fulfilling His foreknowledge.
Notice how this view emphasizes that displeasure with the act is compatible with willing its occurrence for a just purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: أليس أن الله تعالى لا يرضى بالكفر ولا يحبه ويرضى بالإيمان ويحبه، فلما جاز أن يرضى بما لو تحقق لأوجب تجهيله ولا يرضى بما لو تحقق لكان فيه تحقيق علمه؟ فكذا في الإرادة جاز ذلك، قيل: وهذا مثل السؤال الأول أن إرادة تصحيح المحال بما تصور عندكم أنه نظير ذلك المحال.
ثم نقول: إن الأشعري يقول: إن الله تعالى يرضى بوجود الكفر من الكافر قبيحا وكذا يجب وجود الكفر قبيحا وقوله تعالى: {وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الْكُفْرَ} المراد به المؤمنون دون الكفرة حملت على الخصوص بالدليل فعلى هذا اندفع الإلزام، وعلى قول مشايخنا - رحمهم الله - كل واحد منهما أعني الرضا والمحبة لتحقيق ما علم لأن الرضا بالشيء استحسان له وكذا المحبة واستحسان الفعل يخرجه من أن يكون منهيًا، ومن أن يستحق عليه صاحبه العقوبة فلم يستحسن الكفر الذي علم وجوده بل استقبح وكره على ما يقتضى وجوده، إذ هو لن يتصور إلا قبيحا ليكون سببًا للعقوبة وإدخال النار فيتحقق ما علم الله تعالى وما أخبر، والله الموفق.
وتبين بأن هذه الطريقة المحكية عن أبي حنيفة - رحمه الله - هي الدلالة العقلية التي لا يدفعها إلا معاند ولا يأبى قبولها والانقياد لها إلا مكابر، والله ولي التوفيق. فأما تعلقه بالآيات فغير سديد، فأما قوله تعالى: {وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ} فغير دال على ما يزعمون فإن كثيرًا من أهل التأويل قالوا معناه
For they say: 'Does Allah not not be pleased with disbelief nor love it, and be pleased with faith and love it? So how can He be pleased with what, if it occurred, would necessitate making Him ignorant, and not be pleased with what, if it occurred, would contain the realization of His knowledge? And similarly with will, how can that be?' It is said: This is like the first question—the will to affirm the impossible with what you imagine is the like of that impossible.
Then we say: Al-Ash'ari says that Allah is pleased with the existence of disbelief from the disbeliever as ugly, and likewise the existence of disbelief must be ugly. And His saying, the Exalted: {And He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants} means the believers, not the disbelievers; it is carried as specific by evidence. On this the objection is repelled. According to the saying of our shaykhs—may Allah have mercy on them—each one of them, namely pleasure and love, is for the realization of what He knew, because pleasure with a thing is approval of it, and similarly love; and approval of an act removes it from being prohibited and from its doer deserving punishment. So He did not approve of the disbelief whose existence He knew, but rather disapproved and hated it in accordance with what its existence requires, for it cannot be conceived except as ugly, in order to be a cause for punishment and entry into the Fire, so that what Allah knew and informed of is realized. And Allah is the Giver of success.
And it is evident that this method transmitted from Abu Hanifa—may Allah have mercy on him—is the rational proof that none denies except an obstinate opponent, and none refuses to accept it and submit to it except a tyrant. And Allah is the Granter of success. As for his clinging to the verses, it is not sound. As for His saying, the Exalted: {And I created jinn and mankind only to worship Me}, it does not indicate what they claim, for many of the interpreters said its meaning is...
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage addresses a second objection: if God is not pleased with disbelief and loves faith, how can He be pleased with something that necessitates tajhil (making Him ignorant) and not be pleased with something that realizes His knowledge? The author first dismisses it as similar to the first objection. Then he presents two answers. First, the Ash'ari position: God is pleased with disbelief from the disbeliever as ugly (qabih), and the verse 'He is not pleased with disbelief for His servants' refers specifically to believers, not disbelievers, so the objection fails. Second, the Maturidi position (mashayikh): pleasure and love are understood as approval (istihsan), and approval of an act removes it from being prohibited and from its doer deserving punishment. Since God did not approve of the disbelief He knew would occur—rather He disapproved and hated it—there is no contradiction. The disbelief is ugly by nature and serves as a cause for punishment, thereby realizing God's foreknowledge. The passage concludes by affirming that this rational method (al-dalala al-aqliyya), transmitted from Abu Hanifa, is definitive and can only be rejected by obstinate opponents. The author then briefly dismisses an appeal to the Qur'anic verse 'I created jinn and mankind only to worship Me,' noting that many interpreters interpret it in a way that does not support the objector's claim (the interpretation is cut off).

Key Terms
رضا
محبة
إرادة
استحسان
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's pleasure with the existence of disbelief implies moral approval.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God disapproves of disbelief (istqbah) but wills it for a just purpose, as explained by the Maturidi scholars.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi scholars (mashayikh) in this passage, how does God relate to the disbelief that He knows will occur?
A) He is pleased with it because it fulfills His will
B) He is not pleased with it; rather He disapproves and hates it
C) He is both pleased and displeased with it
D) He has no attribute of pleasure or displeasure

Key Takeaway
Both Ash‘ari and Maturidi agree that God does not approve of disbelief, but they differ on how to interpret divine 'pleasure' and the scope of the Qur'anic verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God wills the existence of disbelief yet is not pleased with it, does this challenge your assumptions about His sovereignty and goodness? How can you trust His wisdom when He permits evil that He hates?
?
God disapproves of disbelief, yet it occurs. In your own life, do you ever find yourself tolerating sins you know He disapproves of, relying on His mercy without taking His displeasure seriously? How would your daily choices change if you truly felt His hatred of sin?
?
The Maturidi explanation says disbelief must be ugly to serve as a cause for punishment. Do you view your own sins as 'ugly' in God's sight, or have you become numb to their gravity? What would it mean for you to hate your sin as God does, and then turn to Him for forgiveness?

The Rational Proof of Abu Hanifa
Concluding rational proof from Abu Hanifa and dismissal of a verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hanifa's rational proof is considered definitive and how it dismisses a scriptural objection.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's rational argument is so strong that only an obstinate person would reject it.

After presenting the theological responses, the author concludes by highlighting a rational method transmitted from Abu Hanifa that definitively settles the issue. This lesson focuses on that proof and its epistemic status, as well as the author's initial dismissal of a Qur'anic verse used by opponents.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the claim of rational proof
Read the opening sentence: 'It is evident that this method... is the rational proof.' Notice the strong language: it is unassailable except by the obstinate.
The author asserts that this proof is not merely theological but rationally compelling.
2
Understand why it is definitive
The proof is presented as clear and based on reason; rejection is attributed to obstinacy (inad) or tyranny (mukabara). Reflect on what makes an argument 'rational' in this context.
This reflects the Maturidi emphasis on reason as a primary source of theological knowledge.
3
Note the dismissal of a verse
The author begins to respond to a scriptural argument ('I created jinn and mankind only to worship Me') but the passage cuts off. However, the author states that many interpreters interpret it in a way that does not support the opponent's claim.
This shows that even scriptural arguments can be interpreted in multiple ways, and the rational proof remains decisive.

The Mu'tazila Claim that Allah Does Not Decree Kufr
Mu'tazila claim that Allah does not decree kufr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection to divine decree of evil, specifically their argument that disbelief, being varying and false, cannot be decreed by Allah.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argued that Allah cannot decree evil because evil is false and varying.

The Mu'tazila, a rationalist theological school, raised a powerful objection: if Allah is perfectly good and wise, how can He decree disbelief, which is false and varying? This passage presents their claim concisely, challenging the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on divine decree. We will examine this claim and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once and its translation. Notice the key terms: الكفر, متفاوت, باطل.
The Arabic is short but contains the entire claim.
2
Identify the two reasons
The Mu'tazila give two reasons why Allah does not decree disbelief: it is متفاوت (varying) and باطل (false). Consider what these terms imply about the nature of disbelief.
'Mutfawat' suggests inconsistency or difference in degrees; 'batil' means falsehood.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Think about why the Mu'tazila considered these qualities incompatible with divine decree. How does this objection challenge mainstream Sunni theology?
The refutation is not given here, but the objection itself is important to understand.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المعتزلة أن الله تعالى لا يقضي الكفر لأن الكفر متفاوت باطل.
And the Mu'tazila claimed that Allah the Exalted does not decree disbelief, because disbelief is varying and false.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents a claim by the Mu'tazila that Allah does not decree disbelief (kufr) because disbelief is mutafawat (varying, or differing in degree) and batil (false). The Mu'tazila argued that since disbelief is inherently false and evil, it cannot be attributed to Allah's decree. The text here only states the objection; the author's response is presumably on the following page. This passage is incomplete in this window.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الكفر
متفاوت
باطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila denied divine decree entirely.
Correction: The Mu'tazila specifically denied that Allah decrees evil acts like disbelief, not that He decrees everything.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila claim presented in this passage?
A) Allah decrees everything including disbelief
B) Allah does not decree disbelief because it is varying and false
C) Allah decrees only good actions
D) Allah's decree is irrelevant to human actions

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that disbelief's false and varying nature precludes it from being decreed by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone reject faith, do you ever catch yourself thinking that God must not have willed that? How does the Mu'tazila's reasoning—though incomplete—challenge you to fully accept Allah's decree over all things, even disbelief? Pause right now and silently repeat: 'I believe in Allah's decree, both the good and the bad.' Is that statement true for you?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that kufr cannot be decreed because it is varying and false. Without knowing the orthodox response, does this argument feel appealing to your sense of justice? Examine your heart: Are there aspects of divine decree you resist because they seem unfair? Write down one area where you struggle to submit to Allah's will, and commit to learning the correct Islamic perspective on it this week.
?
This passage only states the Mu'tazila position. But you already know the orthodox view is that Allah decrees everything. Are you truly at peace with that? If you were to die tonight, would you be content that Allah's decree encompasses both the faith and disbelief of every soul? Ask Allah to increase your conviction in qadr and to grant you acceptance of His will, even when you do not fully understand it.

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Guidance as Mere Exposition
Refutes Mu'tazilite view that guidance is mere exposition, using Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite interpretation of guidance as mere exposition is inconsistent with key Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Guidance is not just explanation; it is an effective act of divine creation.

The Mu'tazilites argued that divine guidance is merely explaining the truth. But this passage shows that the Prophet himself could not guide everyone, and that expanding the breast is something only God does. Let's read the Imam's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: refutation, verse, implication.
2
Identify the two Quranic proofs
Find the two Quranic verses used. Notice how each verse is applied to show that guidance cannot be mere exposition.
First verse: Q28:56. Second: Q6:125.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If guidance were only invitation, then everyone invited would have an expanded breast, making the contrary verse false.
This would lead to disbelief, as the author states.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ask yourself: What is at stake in defining guidance? How does this refutation uphold the Ash'ari/Maturidi view of divine creation of actions?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يبطل جميع ما ذهبوا إليه من التأويلات قوله تعالى مخاطبًا لنبيه عليه السلام: إِنَّكَ لَا تَهْدِى مَنْ أَحْبَبْتَ وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ يَهْدِى مَن يَشَاءُ ولو كان الهدى هو البيان لكان النبي عليه السلام يهدي من أحبه، فدل أن وراء البيان هداية أخرى وليس ذلك إلا ما قلنا يدل على أن الله تعالى قال: فَمَن يُرِدِ اللهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ ولو كان المراد من الهداية الدعوة وبيان الطريق ينبغي أن يقال كل من دعاه الله للإيمان وبين له طريق الدين فهو مشروح الصدر، فيصير قوله تعالى: يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا باطلاً كذبًا وهو كفر، وكذا كل من يسميه الله تعالى ضالاً ينبغي أن يجعل صدره ضيقًا حرجًا وكذا كل من يجده ضالاً، ثم إن الله تعالى بين
Then what invalidates all of the interpretations they have resorted to is the statement of God Almighty addressing His Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Indeed, you do not guide whom you love, but God guides whom He wills' (Q28:56). And if guidance were exposition (bayān), the Prophet would guide whom he loves. So it indicates that behind exposition there is another guidance, and that is nothing but what we have said. It indicates that God Almighty said: 'So whoever God wants to guide, He expands his breast for Islam' (Q6:125). And if the intended meaning of guidance were invitation and showing the way, it would be necessary to say that everyone whom God invites to faith and shows the way of religion has an expanded breast. Then the statement of God Almighty 'He makes his breast tight and constricted' would become false and a lie, and that is disbelief. Likewise, everyone whom God Almighty names misguided ought to have his breast made tight and constricted, and likewise everyone whom He finds misguided. Then God Almighty explained...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite interpretation that divine guidance is merely exposition (bayān) or invitation (da'wa). The author cites two Quranic proofs. First, Q28:56, where God tells the Prophet he cannot guide whom he loves. If guidance were just exposition, the Prophet would be able to guide anyone he explained the truth to, but the verse denies this, proving there is a different, effective guidance beyond exposition. Second, Q6:125, where God says He expands the breast of whom He wills to guide. If guidance were only invitation, then everyone invited would have an expanded breast, making the contrasting verse about constriction false and implying God lies, which is disbelief. The same logic applies to misguidance: if God merely names or finds someone misguided, then such a person should have a constricted breast, but the author implies this is not necessarily the case. The refutation thus establishes that guidance and misguidance are real acts of creation by God, not mere labels.

Key Terms
هداية
بيان
دعوة
شرح الصدر
ضيق
حرج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine guidance is purely informational (exposition or invitation) without any effective creation.
Correction: The Quranic verses show that guidance involves an actual change in the person (expansion of breast) and is not merely explaining the truth, as the Prophet himself cannot guide by exposition alone.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is used to argue that guidance cannot be mere exposition?
A) Q28:56
B) Q6:125
C) Q3:173
D) Q9:125

Key Takeaway
The Quran proves that guidance is not mere exposition but a creative act of Allah, which the Mu'tazilite interpretation cannot accommodate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Q28:56, 'You do not guide whom you love, but God guides whom He wills,' do you truly believe that your own efforts to guide others—your words, arguments, or kindness—are ultimately helpless without Allah's direct act? In your heart, do you still rely on your own methods, forgetting that guidance is a gift only He can place? Will you today make a sincere du'a for someone you love to be guided, acknowledging that you cannot force it?
?
The Mu'tazilites reduced guidance to mere explanation, making God's role passive. Examine your own life: have you ever treated the study of Islam as sufficient for guidance—thinking that if you just understand enough, you will be guided? But the lesson shows that even the Prophet's explanation could not guide those he loved. Are there areas of your faith where you have relied on your own comprehension rather than begging Allah to expand your breast? What would change if you desperately sought that expansion?
?
Q6:125 links guidance to a divine act of opening the chest. When you recite this verse, do you feel a sense of dependence on Allah for even the ability to accept truth? Or do you take your own faith for granted, as if it were a natural result of hearing the message? Consider one aspect of deen that you struggle with—prayer, patience, or sincerity. Will you now ask Allah specifically to expand your breast for that struggle, recognizing that only He can create that openness?

Denial of a Special Grace (Luṭf) and Its Consequences
al-Nasafi narrates the Mu'tazila denial of a special grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority Mu'tazila claim that no special grace exists in God's power to cause belief, and the negative attributes they would ascribe to God if He withheld such a grace.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, withholding hypothetical grace makes God foolish, miserly, and unjust.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by presenting the view of the majority of the Mu'tazila on the doctrine of al-Aslah. They first deny that God has a specific grace (luṭf) that could compel disbelievers to believe. Then they argue that if such a grace were in His power and He withheld it, He would be guilty of serious moral failings. This sets up their broader claim about God's obligation to maximize benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The passage uses strong language: 'foolish, miserly, unjust, tyrannical'.
2
Identify the two-part claim
First, the Mu'tazila deny the existence of a grace that would cause belief. Second, they argue that if such a grace existed and was withheld, God would be blameworthy.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this view restricts God's freedom and imposes moral obligations on Him, contrasting with Sunni theology.
This lesson only presents the Mu'tazila view; evaluation comes later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم جمهور المعتزلة أن ليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا، ولو كان ذلك في مقدوره ولم يفعل ولم يعطهم ذلك لكان سفيها بخيلاً جائراً ظالماً مانعا حقا مستحقاً، وغاية ما يقدر الله تعالى عليه مما به صلاح الخلق واجب عليه، وفعل بكل عبد مؤمن أو كافر غاية ما هو في مقدوره من مصلحته، وكما فعل بالنبي عليه السلام ما هو في مقدوره من المصلحة فعل بأبي جهل مثله وليس له على النبي محمد عليه السلام إنعام ليس ذلك على أبي جهل، ولو كان ذلك لكان ظالمًا فيما فعل جائرًا مجابيًا بل فعل غاية ما في مقدوره من مصلحة أبي جهل وليس له أن يفعل بأحد ما هو المفسدة له البتة، هذا هو قول جمهورهم.
The majority of the Mu'tazila claimed that there is not in the power of God Almighty a grace which, if He did to the disbelievers, they would believe. And if that were in His power and He did not do it and did not give it to them, He would be foolish, miserly, unjust, tyrannical, preventing a right that is deserved. The utmost that God Almighty can do that constitutes benefit for creation is obligatory upon Him. He does for every servant, believer or disbeliever, the utmost of what is in His power of benefit for him. Just as He did for the Prophet (peace be upon him) what is in His power of benefit, He did the like for Abu Jahl; He does not have for the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) an act of graciousness that is not also for Abu Jahl. If that were so, He would be unjust in what He did, tyrannical, oppressive. Rather, He does the utmost of what is in His power for Abu Jahl's benefit, and He cannot ever do to anyone that which is harmful to him. This is the saying of their majority.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the majority of the Mu'tazila (Jumhūr al-Mu'tazila) on al-Aslah. They deny that God has a grace (luṭf) in His power that would cause disbelievers to believe willingly—they assert no such grace exists. Moreover, they argue that if such a grace existed and God withheld it, He would be foolish (safīh), miserly (bakhīl), unjust (jā'ir), tyrannical (ẓālim), and preventing a deserved right. They further claim that the utmost benefit that God can provide to creation is obligatory (wājib) upon Him. Consequently, God actually gives every servant—whether believer or disbeliever—the maximum benefit within His power. They draw a striking conclusion: God treats the Prophet and Abu Jahl equally in terms of benefit, with no special favoritism; if there were any extra grace for the Prophet, God would be unjust. Thus, God must give the utmost possible benefit to everyone and can never cause harm. The passage ends by noting that this is the creed of the majority of the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
جمهور المعتزلة
لطف
سفيها
بخيلا
جائراً
ظالماً
حق مستحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila believe God could withhold benefit if He wished.
Correction: The passage shows they argue the opposite: if He could grant a grace and didn't, He would be unjust, so He must not have such a grace in His power.

Checkpoint
According to the majority Mu'tazila, if God had a grace that could make disbelievers believe and He did not give it, what would He be?
A) Wise and merciful
B) Foolish, miserly, unjust, tyrannical
C) Just but less powerful
D) Acting inscrutably

Key Takeaway
The majority Mu'tazila deny that God possesses a grace that would compel belief, and they argue that holding such a grace would force unacceptable descriptions of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God was holding back something that would make your faith easier? How does the Mu'tazila's denial of such a grace challenge you to take responsibility for your own belief—since no excuse of a withheld grace exists? What step will you take today to actively seek faith without waiting for a divine nudge?
?
If you truly believed that God never withholds a grace that would cause belief, would you still make excuses for your sins and shortcomings? Reflect on one area of your life where you blame circumstances or lack of guidance—how can you turn to God now, knowing that He has already given you all that is needed for you to believe and obey? What is stopping you from that turn?

Obligation of Maximum Benefit for All
al-Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila obligation to maximize benefit for all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine that God is obligated to grant the utmost possible benefit to every servant, believer or disbeliever, and that He cannot cause harm.

Core Idea
God must give maximum benefit to all, treating Prophet and sinner equally.

Having denied a special grace, the Mu'tazila affirm a positive obligation: God must do the utmost benefit for every person. They draw a striking parallel between the Prophet Muhammad and Abu Jahl, asserting that God cannot favor one over the other in benefit. This section completes their doctrine of al-Aslah, emphasizing equality in divine provision.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second part of the source text, noting the assertion of obligation and the equal treatment claim.
The comparison between the Prophet and Abu Jahl is the most controversial point.
2
Understand the obligation
The Mu'tazila claim that the utmost benefit (ghāyat al-maṣlaḥa) is obligatory for God. He must do it for each person.
This is an ethical obligation, not a voluntary act.
3
Analyze the equality claim
God treats the Prophet and Abu Jahl equally in benefit, without any special favor. If He did favor, He would be unjust.
This directly challenges the Islamic concept of divine grace and favoritism.
4
Reflect on the prohibition of harm
The passage ends with the absolute prohibition of God causing any harm to anyone.
This follows from the obligation to maximize benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم جمهور المعتزلة أن ليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا، ولو كان ذلك في مقدوره ولم يفعل ولم يعطهم ذلك لكان سفيها بخيلاً جائراً ظالماً مانعا حقا مستحقاً، وغاية ما يقدر الله تعالى عليه مما به صلاح الخلق واجب عليه، وفعل بكل عبد مؤمن أو كافر غاية ما هو في مقدوره من مصلحته، وكما فعل بالنبي عليه السلام ما هو في مقدوره من المصلحة فعل بأبي جهل مثله وليس له على النبي محمد عليه السلام إنعام ليس ذلك على أبي جهل، ولو كان ذلك لكان ظالمًا فيما فعل جائرًا مجابيًا بل فعل غاية ما في مقدوره من مصلحة أبي جهل وليس له أن يفعل بأحد ما هو المفسدة له البتة، هذا هو قول جمهورهم.
The majority of the Mu'tazila claimed that there is not in the power of God Almighty a grace which, if He did to the disbelievers, they would believe. And if that were in His power and He did not do it and did not give it to them, He would be foolish, miserly, unjust, tyrannical, preventing a right that is deserved. The utmost that God Almighty can do that constitutes benefit for creation is obligatory upon Him. He does for every servant, believer or disbeliever, the utmost of what is in His power of benefit for him. Just as He did for the Prophet (peace be upon him) what is in His power of benefit, He did the like for Abu Jahl; He does not have for the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) an act of graciousness that is not also for Abu Jahl. If that were so, He would be unjust in what He did, tyrannical, oppressive. Rather, He does the utmost of what is in His power for Abu Jahl's benefit, and He cannot ever do to anyone that which is harmful to him. This is the saying of their majority.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the majority of the Mu'tazila (Jumhūr al-Mu'tazila) on al-Aslah. They deny that God has a grace (luṭf) in His power that would cause disbelievers to believe willingly—they assert no such grace exists. Moreover, they argue that if such a grace existed and God withheld it, He would be foolish (safīh), miserly (bakhīl), unjust (jā'ir), tyrannical (ẓālim), and preventing a deserved right. They further claim that the utmost benefit that God can provide to creation is obligatory (wājib) upon Him. Consequently, God actually gives every servant—whether believer or disbeliever—the maximum benefit within His power. They draw a striking conclusion: God treats the Prophet and Abu Jahl equally in terms of benefit, with no special favoritism; if there were any extra grace for the Prophet, God would be unjust. Thus, God must give the utmost possible benefit to everyone and can never cause harm. The passage ends by noting that this is the creed of the majority of the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
غاية المصلحة
أبو جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Mu'tazila allow God to harm if He wills.
Correction: They explicitly state God cannot ever cause harm (mafsada).

Checkpoint
According to the majority Mu'tazila, what would God be if He gave the Prophet a special benefit not given to Abu Jahl?
A) Merciful
B) Unjust
C) Wise
D) Generous

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila hold that God is obligated to give every person the maximum possible benefit, without any special favor, and cannot cause harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God treats the Prophet and Abu Jahl equally in terms of benefit, what does that say about your own expectation that your faith deserves special rewards? Do you serve God expecting payback, or simply because He is God?
?
The Mu'tazila claim God cannot cause harm. Think of a time you suffered deeply. Did you blame God or feel He was unfair? How does this passage challenge you to see hardship differently—perhaps not as harm from God but as something else?
?
If you truly believe that God gives the same maximum benefit to every person, including your worst enemy, how would that change the way you treat that enemy? Would you be able to let go of resentment and see them as equally cared for by God?

Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's View on Divine Grace and His Retraction
Imam al-Nasafi recounts Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's theory on divine grace and his retraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's distinction between obligatory benefit and non-obligatory optimal benefit, and his reasoning that obligating the most beneficial would imply a limit on God's power.

Core Idea
God must provide benefit but is not obligated to give the best—otherwise His power would be limited.

Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, a leading Mu'tazilite of Baghdad, developed a nuanced position on divine grace that distinguished him from his peers. While affirming that God must do what is beneficial for His servants, he denied that God is obligated to grant the most beneficial, because that would impose a limit on divine power. This passage presents his view, its justification, and a later report of his retraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Bishr carefully distinguishes between different types of benefit.
2
Identify the three elements of Bishr's view
Identify the three claims: (1) God must do what is beneficial, not harmful; (2) God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial; (3) God is capable of a special grace that would cause faith, but is not required to give it.
Each claim is separate and has its own reasoning.
3
Understand the reasoning against obligating the most beneficial
Bishr argues that if God were obligated to give the most beneficial, that would imply a limit to His power—since there would be a maximum benefit beyond which He cannot go. That is impossible for God.
This is the core of Bishr's departure from the majority Mu'tazila.
4
Note the historical footnote about retraction
The passage ends with a report from al-Ka'bi that Bishr later repented and returned to the majority view. This is important for understanding the reception of his ideas.
The text cuts off, but the main point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بشر بن المعتمر رئيس معتزلة بغداد ومن تابعه من أصحابه: إن الله تعالى لا يجوز أن يفعل بعبده ما هو المفسدة له بل يجب عليه أن يفعل به ما هو المصلحة له لكن لا يجب عليه أن يفعل ما هو الأصلح إذ ليس لما في مقدور الله تعالى من المصلحة واللطف غاية لما في القول بإيجاب الأصلح القول بتناهي مقدور الله تعالى: وذلك محال بل هو تعالى قادر على لطف لو فعل لأمنوا اختيارا بهم إيمانًا يستحقون على الله تعالى الثواب مثل ما يستحقونه لو آمنوا مع عدمه، ولا يجب عليه إعطاء ذلك اللطف، وإنما يجب عليه إعطاء ما هو صلاح لهم وإزاحة عللهم فيما يحتاجون إليه لأداء ما كلفهم وما تيسر عليهم مع وجوده العمل بما أمرهم به، وقد فعل ذلك بهم. هذا هو المشهور من مذهبه ومذهب أتباعه، وعامة المعتزلة يسمونهم أصحاب اللطف. وذكر الكعبي في كتابه المقالات أنه تاب عن هذا ورجع إلى قول أصحابه قال: كتب إلى ذلك أبو الحسين الخياط، وحكاه عن بعض البصريين عن الشحام عن
And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the head of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad, and those of his companions who followed him, said: Verily God Almighty is not permitted to do to His servant that which is harmful to him; rather, He must do for him what is beneficial (al-maṣlaḥa) for him, but He is not obligated to do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), because there is no limit to what is in God's power of benefit and grace. For the statement that obligates the most beneficial implies the finitude of God's power — and that is impossible. Rather, He is Almighty, powerful over a grace which, if He did it, they would believe willingly, having faith for which they deserve reward from God Almighty like the reward they would deserve if they believed without it. It is not obligatory upon Him to give that grace; rather, it is obligatory upon Him to give what is beneficial (ṣalāḥ) for them and to remove their excuses in matters they need for performing what He has obligated them, and which facilitate obedience to His commands. And He has done that for them. This is the well-known position of his school and the school of his followers; and the general body of the Mu'tazila call them 'the people of grace' (aṣḥāb al-luṭf). Al-Ka'bi mentioned in his book 'al-Maqālāt' that he (Bishr) repented of this view and returned to the position of his companions. He said: Abu al-Husayn al-Khayyāṭ wrote to him about that, and he related it from some of the Basrans from al-Shahḥām from...
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, a leading figure of the Baghdad Mu'tazila, along with his followers. Bishr's position distinguishes itself from the majority Mu'tazila. He affirms that God cannot do what is harmful (mafsada) to a servant; rather, God must do what is beneficial (maṣlaḥa) for him. However, unlike the majority, Bishr denies that God is obligated to provide the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ). His reasoning is that if God were obliged to give the most beneficial, that would imply a limit to God's power (tanāhī muqdūrihī), which is impossible—since God's power is infinite, there is always a possible greater benefit. He further affirms that God is capable of a specific grace (luṭf) that would cause disbelievers to believe willingly, earning reward equal to that which they would have earned without that grace. Yet, God is not obligated to give that grace; His obligation is only to provide what is necessary for fulfilling religious duties and to remove excuses. The passage adds that this view is the well-known doctrine of Bishr and his followers, who are called 'aṣḥāb al-luṭf' by other Mu'tazila. Finally, a historical note from al-Ka'bi's 'al-Maqālāt' states that Bishr later repented and returned to the majority view, as reported by al-Khayyāṭ through a chain of transmission. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
بشر بن المعتمر
المفسدة
المصلحة
الأصلح
اللطف
تناهي مقدور الله
أصحاب اللطف
الكعبي
المقالات
أبو الحسين الخياط
الشحام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bishr held the same view as the majority Mu'tazila regarding God's obligation to provide the most beneficial.
Correction: Bishr distinguished between obligatory benefit and non-obligatory optimal benefit, denying that God must give the most beneficial.

Checkpoint
Why did Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir argue that God is not obligated to give the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ)?
A) Because God does not have the power to give the most beneficial
B) Because obligating the most beneficial would imply a limit to God's power
C) Because the most beneficial is harmful to the servant
D) Because God does not care about human benefit

Key Takeaway
Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir argued that God must provide benefit but is not obligated to give the most beneficial, because that would imply a limit on God's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God owes you the best outcome in a situation, and when it didn't come, you questioned His goodness? Bishr's argument reminds us that Allah is not obligated to give the 'most beneficial'—only what is genuinely beneficial. Can you identify a specific disappointment in your life that you now realize was still a benefit from Allah, even if it wasn't the best? Repent of any entitlement and thank Him for His perfect provision.
?
When hardship strikes, do you instinctively think Allah has failed to give you what is truly beneficial (maṣlaḥa)? This lesson teaches that Allah must provide benefit, but that benefit may include trials that remove excuses and facilitate obedience. Consider a current trial: How might it be serving as a 'benefit' by strengthening your faith or removing your excuses? Write down one concrete way you can respond to it with gratitude and submission.
?
Bishr himself repented from his view after being confronted with evidence. Are you holding onto a theological opinion or assumption about Allah that you have never seriously re-examined? This lesson invites you to search your heart: Is there any belief you cling to that might compromise Allah's absolute sovereignty or your trust in His unlimited power? Commit to studying the evidence and being willing to repent, just as Bishr did.
